<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Christopher Columbus</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Christopher Columbus</b> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Cristoforo Colombo</i></span> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[kriˈstɔːforo koˈlombo]</a></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1451</span> – 20 May 1506) was an <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italian</a> explorer, navigator, and <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonizer</a>. Born in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741-4">[3]</a></sup> under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spain</a> he completed <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">four voyages</a> across the Atlantic Ocean. Those voyages and his efforts to establish settlements on the island of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> initiated the permanent <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>.</p>
<p>At a time when <a href="/wiki/Monarchies_in_Europe" title="Monarchies in Europe">European kingdoms</a> were beginning to establish new <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> and colonies, motivated by <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">economic competition</a>, Columbus proposed to reach the <a href="/wiki/East_Indies" title="East Indies">East Indies</a> (<a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>) by sailing westward. This eventually received the support of the Spanish Crown, which saw a chance to enter the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> with Asia through this new route. During his first voyage in 1492, he reached the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">New World</a> instead of arriving in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> as he had intended, landing on an island in the <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">Bahamas archipelago</a> that he named <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador_Island" title="San Salvador Island">San Salvador</a>. Over the course of three more voyages, he visited the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Antilles" title="Greater Antilles">Greater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, claiming all of it for the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Crown of Castile</a>.</p>
<p>Though preceded by short-lived <a href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_North_America" title="Norse colonization of North America">Norse colonization of North America</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Leif_Erikson" title="Leif Erikson">Leif Erikson</a> in the 11th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> Columbus is the European explorer credited with establishing and documenting routes to the Americas, <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">securing lasting European ties to the Americas</a>, and inaugurating a period of exploration, conquest, and colonization that lasted for centuries. His exertions thereby strongly contributed to the development of the modern Western world. He also founded the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">transatlantic slave trade</a> and has been accused by several historians of initiating the <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> of the Hispaniola natives. Columbus himself saw his accomplishments primarily in the light of <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">spreading the Christian religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-online_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-online-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus had set course in hopes of finding a western route to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">Indies</a> (Asia). He called the inhabitants of the lands that he visited <i>indios</i> (Spanish for "Indians").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoxie_1996_p._8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoxie_1996_p.-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbst1997_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbst1997-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilton2004_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilton2004-10">[9]</a></sup> His strained relationship with the Spanish crown and its appointed colonial administrators in America led to his arrest and dismissal as governor of the settlements on the island of Hispaniola in 1500, and later to protracted litigation over the benefits that he and his heirs claimed were owed to them by the crown.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For more details on Columbus's birthplace and family background, see <a href="/wiki/Origin_theories_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Origin theories of Christopher Columbus">Origin theories of Christopher Columbus</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2881" data-file-width="2113" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg/220px-Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg/330px-Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg/440px-Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_at_the_gates_of_the_monastery_of_Santa_Maria_de_la_Rabida_with_his_son_Diego.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Christopher Columbus at the gates of the <a href="/wiki/La_R%C3%A1bida_Friary" title="La Rábida Friary">monastery of Santa María de la Rábida</a> with his son Diego</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Benet_Mercad%C3%A9" title="Benet Mercadé">Benet Mercadé</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The name <i>Christopher Columbus</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">Anglicisation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <b>Christophorus Columbus</b>. His name in Italian is <b>Cristoforo Colombo</b> and, in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, it is <b>Cristóbal Colón</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741-4">[3]</a></sup> He was born before 31 October 1451 in the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a> (now part of modern Italy), though the exact location remains disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr19929_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr19929-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[b]</a></sup> His father was <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Colombo" title="Domenico Colombo">Domenico Colombo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741-4">[3]</a></sup> a middle-class wool weaver who worked both in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a> and who also owned a cheese stand at which young Christopher worked as a helper. His mother was <a href="/wiki/Susanna_Fontanarossa" title="Susanna Fontanarossa">Susanna Fontanarossa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741-4">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Columbus" title="Bartholomew Columbus">Bartolomeo</a>, Giovanni Pellegrino, and Giacomo were his brothers. Bartolomeo worked in a <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> workshop in <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> for at least part of his adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-13">[11]</a></sup> He also had a sister named Bianchinetta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus never wrote in his native language, which is presumed to have been a <a href="/wiki/Genoese_dialect" title="Genoese dialect">Genoese variety of Ligurian</a> (his name would translate in the 16th-century Genoese language as <i>Christoffa</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> <i>Corombo</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> <small>Ligurian pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[kriˈʃtɔffa kuˈɹuŋbu]</a></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup>). In one of his writings, he says he went to sea at the age of 10. In 1470, the Columbus family moved to <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a>, where Domenico took over a tavern. In the same year, Christopher was on a Genoese ship hired in the service of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_of_Anjou" title="René of Anjou">René of Anjou</a> to support his attempt to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">Kingdom of Naples</a>. Some modern historians have argued that he was not from Genoa but, instead, from the <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> or from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> These competing hypotheses have generally been discounted by mainstream scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson19973_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson19973-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr199285_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr199285-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1334" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/240px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/360px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/480px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus's handwritten notes in Latin, on the margins of his copy of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1473, Columbus began his apprenticeship as business agent for the important <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Centurione&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Centurione (page does not exist)">Centurione</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Di_Negro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Di Negro (page does not exist)">Di Negro</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinola_family" title="Spinola family">Spinola</a> families of Genoa. Later, he allegedly made a trip to <a href="/wiki/Chios" title="Chios">Chios</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> island then ruled by Genoa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> In May 1476, he took part in an armed convoy sent by Genoa to carry valuable cargo to northern Europe. He docked in <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>, England<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Galway" title="Galway">Galway</a>, Ireland. In 1477, he was possibly in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeazley1911741-4">[3]</a></sup> In the autumn of 1477, he sailed on a Portuguese ship from Galway to Lisbon, where he found his brother Bartolomeo, and they continued trading for the Centurione family. Columbus based himself in Lisbon from 1477 to 1485. He married <a href="/wiki/Filipa_Moniz_Perestrelo" title="Filipa Moniz Perestrelo">Filipa Moniz Perestrelo</a>, daughter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porto_Santo" title="Porto Santo">Porto Santo</a> governor and Portuguese nobleman of <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombard</a> origin <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Perestrello" title="Bartolomeu Perestrello">Bartolomeu Perestrello</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1479 or 1480, his son <a href="/wiki/Diego_Columbus" title="Diego Columbus">Diego Columbus</a> was born. Between 1482 and 1485, Columbus traded along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, reaching the Portuguese trading post of <a href="/wiki/Elmina" title="Elmina">Elmina</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_coast" title="Guinea coast">Guinea coast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-online_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-online-7">[6]</a></sup> Some records report that Filipa died sometime around 1485, while Columbus was away in Castile. He returned to Portugal to settle her estate and take his son Diego with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> He had left Portugal for <a href="/wiki/Castile_(historical_region)" title="Castile (historical region)">Castile</a> in 1485, where he found a mistress in 1487, a 20-year-old orphan named <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_Enr%C3%ADquez_de_Arana" title="Beatriz Enríquez de Arana">Beatriz Enríquez de Arana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> It is likely that Beatriz met Columbus when he was in <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, a gathering site of many Genoese merchants and where the court of the Catholic monarchs was located at intervals. Beatriz, unmarried at the time, gave birth to Columbus's natural son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fernando_Columbus" title="Fernando Columbus">Fernando Columbus</a> in July 1488, named for the monarch of Aragón. Columbus recognized the boy as his offspring. Columbus entrusted his older, legitimate son Diego to take care of Beatriz and pay the pension set aside for her following his death, but Diego was negligent in his duties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ambitious, Columbus eventually learned Latin, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, and Castilian. He read widely about astronomy, geography, and history, including the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Claudius Ptolemy">Claudius Ptolemy</a>, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Pierre_d%27Ailly" title="Pierre d'Ailly">Pierre d'Ailly</a>'s <i>Imago Mundi</i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="Travels of Marco Polo">travels of Marco Polo</a> and Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Mandeville" title="John Mandeville">John Mandeville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_II" title="Pope Pius II">Pope Pius II</a>'s <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Historia_Rerum_Ubique_Gestarum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Historia Rerum Ubique Gestarum (page does not exist)">Historia Rerum Ubique Gestarum</a></i>. According to historian <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Morgan_(historian)" title="Edmund Morgan (historian)">Edmund Morgan</a>,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Columbus was not a scholarly man. Yet he studied these books, made hundreds of marginal notations in them and came out with ideas about the world that were characteristically simple and strong and sometimes wrong, ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESMorgan_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESMorgan-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Throughout his life, Columbus also showed a keen interest in the Bible and in <a href="/wiki/Bible_prophecy" title="Bible prophecy">Biblical prophecies</a>, often quoting biblical texts in his letters and logs. For example, part of the argument that he submitted to the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> when he sought their support for his proposed expedition to reach the Indies by sailing west was based on his reading of the <a href="/wiki/2_Esdras" title="2 Esdras">Second Book of Esdras</a> (<a href="/wiki/Esdras" title="Esdras">Ezra</a>): see <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblestudytools.com/tmba/2-esdras/6-42.html" rel="nofollow">2 Esdras 6:42</a>, which he took to mean that the Earth is made of six parts of land to one of water. Towards the end of his life, he produced a <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Prophecies" title="Book of Prophecies">Book of Prophecies</a></i> in which his career as an explorer is interpreted in the light of <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalypticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest_for_Asia">Quest for Asia</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColombusMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1260" data-file-width="1970" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/350px-ColombusMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/525px-ColombusMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/ColombusMap.jpg/700px-ColombusMap.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColombusMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Columbus map", drawn c. 1490 in the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> workshop of Bartolomeo and Christopher Columbus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>'s hegemony over Asia (the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i>, or <i>Mongol peace</i>), Europeans had long enjoyed <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">a safe land passage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">Indies</a> (then construed roughly as all of south and east Asia) and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, which were sources of valuable goods such as spices and silk. With the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">fall of Constantinople</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> in 1453, the land route to Asia became much more difficult and dangerous. Portuguese navigators tried to find a sea way to Asia.</p>
<p>In 1470, the Florentine astronomer <a href="/wiki/Paolo_dal_Pozzo_Toscanelli" title="Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli">Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli</a> suggested to King <a href="/wiki/Afonso_V_of_Portugal" title="Afonso V of Portugal">Afonso V of Portugal</a> that sailing west would be a quicker way to reach the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Spice Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cipangu" title="Cipangu">Cipangu</a> than the route around Africa. Afonso rejected his proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> Portuguese explorers, under the leadership of King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II</a>, then developed the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Route" title="Cape Route">Cape Route</a> to Asia around Africa. Major progress in this quest was achieved in 1488, when <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> reached the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, in what is now South Africa. Meanwhile, in the 1480s, the Columbus brothers had picked up Toscanelli's suggestion and proposed a plan to reach the Indies by sailing west across the "Ocean Sea", i.e., the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>. However, Dias's discovery had shifted the interests of Portuguese seafaring to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_passage" title="Southeast passage">southeast passage</a>, which complicated Columbus's proposals significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_considerations">Geographical considerations</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Washington_Irving" title="Washington Irving">Washington Irving</a>'s 1828 biography of Columbus popularized the idea that Columbus had difficulty obtaining support for his plan because many Catholic theologians insisted that the <a href="/wiki/Flat_Earth" title="Flat Earth">Earth was flat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-book3-33">[31]</a></sup> In fact, nearly all educated Westerners had understood, at least since the time of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_Flat_Earth" title="Myth of the Flat Earth">the Earth is spherical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> The sphericity of the Earth is also accounted for in the work of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, on which <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">medieval astronomy</a> was largely based. Christian writers whose works clearly reflect the conviction that the Earth is spherical include Saint <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> the Venerable in his <i>Reckoning of Time</i>, written around AD 723. In Columbus's time, the techniques of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a>, which use the position of the sun and the stars in the sky, together with the understanding that the Earth is a sphere, had long been in use by astronomers and were beginning to be implemented by mariners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>As far back as the 3rd century BC, <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> had correctly computed the circumference of the Earth by using simple geometry and studying the shadows cast by objects at two different locations: <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and Syene (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan_40.2C04147_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sagan_40.2C04147-36">[34]</a></sup> Eratosthenes's results were confirmed by a comparison of stellar observations at Alexandria and <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>, carried out by <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a> in the 1st century BC. These measurements were widely known among scholars, but confusion about the old-fashioned units of distance in which they were expressed had led, in Columbus's day, to some debate about the exact size of the Earth.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean,_Toscanelli,_1474.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1215" data-file-width="1990" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/300px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/450px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg/600px-Atlantic_Ocean%2C_Toscanelli%2C_1474.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean,_Toscanelli,_1474.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Paolo_dal_Pozzo_Toscanelli" title="Paolo dal Pozzo Toscanelli">Toscanelli</a>'s notions of the geography of the Atlantic Ocean (shown superimposed on a modern map), which directly influenced Columbus's plans.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From d'Ailly's <i>Imago Mundi</i> Columbus learned of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Muhammad_ibn_Kath%C4%ABr_al-Fargh%C4%81n%C4%AB" title="Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Kathīr al-Farghānī">Alfraganus</a>'s estimate that a degree of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> (or a degree of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> along the equator) spanned 56⅔ miles, but did not realize that this was expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_mile" title="Arabic mile">Arabic mile</a> rather than the shorter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_mile" title="Roman mile">Roman mile</a> with which he was familiar (1,480 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> He therefore estimated the circumference of the Earth to be about 30,200 km, whereas the correct value is 40,000 km (25,000 mi).</p>
<p>Furthermore, most scholars accepted <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s estimate that <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> spanned 180° <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, rather than the actual 130° (to the Chinese mainland) or 150° (to Japan at the latitude of Spain). Columbus, for his part, believed the even higher estimate of <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus of Tyre</a>, which put the longitudinal span of the Eurasian landmass at 225°, leaving only 135° of water. He also believed that Japan (which he called "Cipangu", following <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>) was much larger, farther to the east from China ("Cathay"), and closer to the equator than it is, and that there were inhabited islands even farther to the east than Japan, including the mythical <a href="/wiki/Antillia" title="Antillia">Antillia</a>, which he thought might lie not much farther to the west than the <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">Azores</a>. In this, he was influenced by the ideas of Florentine astronomer Toscanelli, who corresponded with Columbus before his death in 1482 and who also defended the feasibility of a westward route to Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison-Admiral_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-Admiral-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus therefore estimated the distance from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> to Japan to be about 3,000 Italian miles (3,700 km, or 2,300 statute miles). The true figure is now known to be vastly larger: about 20,000 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992110_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992110-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[c]</a></sup> No ship in the 15th century could have carried enough food and fresh water for such a long voyage, and the dangers involved in navigating through the uncharted ocean would have been formidable. Most European navigators reasonably concluded that a westward voyage from Europe to Asia was unfeasible. The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a>, however, having completed <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">an expensive war</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, were eager to obtain a competitive edge over other European countries in the quest for trade with the Indies. Columbus's project, though far-fetched, held the promise of such an advantage.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nautical_considerations">Nautical considerations</span></h3>
<p>Though Columbus was wrong about the number of degrees of longitude that separated Europe from the Far East and about the distance that each degree represented, he did possess valuable knowledge about the <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a>, which would prove to be the key to his successful navigation of the Atlantic Ocean. During his first voyage in 1492, the brisk trade winds from the east, commonly called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Easterlies" title="Easterlies">easterlies</a>", propelled Columbus's fleet for five weeks, from the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> to <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">The Bahamas</a>. The precise first land sighting and landing point was <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador_Island" title="San Salvador Island">San Salvador Island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> To return to Spain against this prevailing wind would have required several months of an arduous sailing technique, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close_hauled#Close_hauled" title="Close hauled">beating</a>, during which food and drinkable water would probably have been exhausted.</p>
<p>Instead, Columbus returned home by following the curving trade winds northeastward to the middle latitudes of the North Atlantic, where he was able to catch the "<a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a>" that blow eastward to the coast of Western Europe. There, in turn, the winds curve southward towards the Iberian Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is unclear whether Columbus learned about the winds from his own sailing experience or if he had heard about them from others. The corresponding technique for efficient travel in the Atlantic appears to have been exploited first by the Portuguese, who referred to it as the <i><a href="/wiki/Volta_do_mar" title="Volta do mar">Volta do mar</a></i> ("turn of the sea"). Columbus's knowledge of the Atlantic wind patterns was, however, imperfect at the time of his first voyage. By sailing directly due west from the Canary Islands during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic_hurricane_seasons" title="List of Atlantic hurricane seasons">hurricane season</a>, skirting the so-called <a href="/wiki/Horse_latitudes" title="Horse latitudes">horse latitudes</a> of the mid-Atlantic, Columbus risked either being becalmed or running into a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a>, both of which, by chance, he avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison-Admiral_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-Admiral-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest_for_financial_support_for_a_voyage">Quest for financial support for a voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_(cropped).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="415" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/170px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/255px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png/340px-Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_%28cropped%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_offers_his_services_to_the_King_of_Portugal_-_Daniel_Nicholas_Chodowiecki_(cropped).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Columbus offers his services to the King of Portugal</i>; <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Chodowiecki" title="Daniel Chodowiecki">Chodowiecki</a>, 17th c.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1485, Columbus presented his plans to King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a>. He proposed that the king equip three sturdy ships and grant Columbus one year's time to sail out into the Atlantic, search for a western route to the <a href="/wiki/Orient" title="Orient">Orient</a>, and return. Columbus also requested he be made "Great Admiral of the Ocean", appointed governor of any and all lands he discovered, and given one-tenth of all revenue from those lands. The king submitted Columbus's proposal to his experts, who rejected it. It was their considered opinion that Columbus's estimation of a travel distance of 2,400 miles (3,860 km) was, in fact, far too low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison-Admiral_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-Admiral-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1488, Columbus appealed to the court of Portugal once again and, once again, John II invited him to an audience. That meeting also proved unsuccessful, in part because not long afterwards <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a> returned to Portugal with news of his successful rounding of the southern tip of Africa (near the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>). With an eastern sea route to Asia apparently at hand, King John was no longer interested in Columbus's far-fetched project.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1147" data-file-width="1536" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG/220px-Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG/330px-Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG/440px-Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze_-_Columbus_Before_the_Queen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Columbus before the Queen</i>, as imagined<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emanuel_Gottlieb_Leutze" title="Emanuel Gottlieb Leutze">Emanuel Gottlieb Leutze</a>, 1843</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Columbus traveled from Portugal to both <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a>, but he received encouragement from neither. He had also dispatched his brother <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Columbus" title="Bartholomew Columbus">Bartholomew</a> to the court of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a> to inquire whether the English crown might sponsor his expedition, but also without success.</p>
<p>Columbus had sought an audience from the monarchs <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>, who had united several kingdoms in the Iberian Peninsula by marrying and were ruling together. On 1 May 1486, permission having been granted, Columbus presented his plans to Queen Isabella, who, in turn, referred it to a committee. After the passing of much time, the <a href="/wiki/Expert" title="Expert">savants</a> of Spain, like their counterparts in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>, replied that Columbus had grossly underestimated the distance to Asia. They pronounced the idea impractical and advised their Royal Highnesses to pass on the proposed venture.</p>
<p>However, to keep Columbus from taking his ideas elsewhere, and perhaps to keep their options open, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">Catholic Monarchs</a> gave him an annual allowance of 12,000 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maravedis" title="Maravedis">maravedis</a></i> and, in 1489, furnished him with a letter ordering all cities and towns under their domain to provide him food and lodging at no cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agreement_with_the_Spanish_crown">Agreement with the Spanish crown</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="1521" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg/330px-Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg/495px-Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg/660px-Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_Fleet_1893_Issue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Flagship of Columbus and the Fleet of Columbus. 400th Anniversary Issues of 1893. (<a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus#Voyages_and_events" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">On ships.</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After continually lobbying at the <a href="/wiki/Cortes_Generales" title="Cortes Generales">Spanish court</a> and two years of negotiations, he finally had success in January 1492. Ferdinand and Isabella had just conquered <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">last Muslim stronghold</a> on the Iberian Peninsula, and they received Columbus in <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Alc%C3%A1zar_de_los_Reyes_Cristianos" title="Alcázar de los Reyes Cristianos">Alcázar</a></i> castle. Isabella turned him down on the advice of her <a href="/wiki/Confessor" title="Confessor">confessor</a>. Columbus was leaving town by mule in despair when Ferdinand intervened. Isabella then sent a royal guard to fetch him, and Ferdinand later claimed credit for being "the principal cause why those islands were discovered".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992132_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992132-45">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the April 1492 "<a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capitulations of Santa Fe">Capitulations of Santa Fe</a>", King Ferdinand and Queen Isabella promised Columbus that if he succeeded he would be given the rank of Admiral of the Ocean Sea and appointed Viceroy and Governor of all the new lands he could claim for Spain. He had the right to nominate three persons, from whom the sovereigns would choose one, for any office in the new lands. He would be entitled to 10 percent of all the revenues from the new lands in perpetuity. Additionally, he would also have the option of buying one-eighth interest in any commercial venture with the new lands and receive one-eighth of the profits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison-Admiral_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-Admiral-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus was later arrested in 1500 and dismissed from his posts. He and his sons, Diego and Fernando, then conducted a lengthy series of court cases against the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castilian crown</a>, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pleitos_colombinos" title="Pleitos colombinos">pleitos colombinos</a></i>, alleging that the Crown had illegally reneged on its contractual obligations to Columbus and his heirs. The Columbus family had some success in their first litigation, as a judgment of 1511 confirmed Diego's position as Viceroy, but reduced his powers. Diego resumed litigation in 1512, which lasted until 1536, and further disputes continued until 1790.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyages">Voyages</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">Voyages of Christopher Columbus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1149" data-file-width="1715" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/350px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/525px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/700px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The voyages of Christopher Columbus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between 1492 and 1503, Columbus completed four round-trip voyages between Spain and the Americas, each voyage being sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Crown of Castile</a>. These voyages marked the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_ethnic_groups" title="European ethnic groups">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">exploration</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_colonialism" title="History of colonialism">colonization</a> of <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">the American continents</a>, and are thus of enormous significance in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Western_civilization" title="History of Western civilization">Western history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-13">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus always insisted, in the face of mounting evidence to the contrary, that the lands that he visited during those voyages were part of the <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> continent, as previously described by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and other European travelers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-13">[11]</a></sup> Columbus's refusal to accept that the lands he had visited and claimed for Spain were not part of Asia might explain, in part, why the American continent was named after the <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a> and not after Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_voyage">First voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_first_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="597" data-file-width="1001" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Columbus_first_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_first_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_first_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First voyage. Modern place names in black, Columbus's place names in blue</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the evening of 3 August 1492, Columbus departed from <a href="/wiki/Palos_de_la_Frontera" title="Palos de la Frontera">Palos de la Frontera</a> with three ships: a larger <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">carrack</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa María (ship)">Santa María</a></i> ex-<i>Gallega</i> ("Galician"), and two smaller <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravels</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pinta_(ship)" title="Pinta (ship)">Pinta</a></i> ("The Pint", "The Look", or "The Spotted One") and the <i>Santa Clara</i>, nicknamed the <i><a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1a" title="Niña">Niña</a></i> ("Girl") after her owner Juan Niño of Moguer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> The monarchs forced the citizens of Palos to contribute to the expedition. The <i>Santa María</i> was owned by <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_la_Cosa" title="Juan de la Cosa">Juan de la Cosa</a> and captained by Columbus. The <i>Pinta</i> and the <i>Niña</i> were piloted by the <a href="/wiki/Pinz%C3%B3n_brothers" title="Pinzón brothers">Pinzón brothers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_Alonso_Pinz%C3%B3n" title="Martín Alonso Pinzón">Martín Alonso</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Y%C3%A1%C3%B1ez_Pinz%C3%B3n" title="Vicente Yáñez Pinzón">Vicente Yáñez</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus first sailed to the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, which belonged to <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castile</a>. He restocked provisions and made repairs in <a href="/wiki/Gran_Canaria" title="Gran Canaria">Gran Canaria</a>, then departed from <a href="/wiki/San_Sebasti%C3%A1n_de_La_Gomera" title="San Sebastián de La Gomera">San Sebastián de La Gomera</a> on 6 September, for what turned out to be a five-week voyage across the ocean. At about 2:00 in the morning of 12 October (21 October, Gregorian Calendar New Style), a lookout on the <i>Pinta</i>, <a href="/wiki/Rodrigo_de_Triana" title="Rodrigo de Triana">Rodrigo de Triana</a> (also known as Juan Rodríguez Bermeo), spotted land, and immediately alerted the rest of the crew with a shout. Thereupon, the captain of the <i>Pinta</i>, Martín Alonso Pinzón, verified the discovery and alerted Columbus by firing a <a href="/wiki/Lombard_(gun)" title="Lombard (gun)">lombard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> Columbus later maintained that he himself had already seen a light on the land a few hours earlier, thereby claiming for himself the lifetime pension promised by Ferdinand and Isabella to the first person to sight land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus called the island (in what is now <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">the Bahamas</a>) <i>San Salvador</i> (meaning "Holy Savior"); the natives called it <a href="/wiki/Guanahani" title="Guanahani">Guanahani</a>. Exactly which island in the Bahamas this corresponds to is unresolved. Based on primary accounts and on what one would expect from the geographic positions of the islands given Columbus's course, the prime candidates are <a href="/wiki/San_Salvador_Island" title="San Salvador Island">San Salvador Island</a> (so named in 1925 on the theory that it was Columbus's San Salvador),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samana_Cay" title="Samana Cay">Samana Cay</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plana_Cays" title="Plana Cays">Plana Cays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_Columbus_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1971" data-file-width="3000" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/220px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/330px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg/440px-Landing_of_Columbus_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Landing_of_Columbus_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Landing of Columbus</i> (12 October 1492), painting by <a href="/wiki/John_Vanderlyn" title="John Vanderlyn">John Vanderlyn</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous Peoples of the Americas">indigenous people</a> he encountered, the <a href="/wiki/Lucayan_people" title="Lucayan people">Lucayan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" title="Taíno people">Taíno</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arawak_peoples" title="Arawak peoples">Arawak</a>, were peaceful and friendly. Noting their gold ear ornaments, Columbus took some of the Arawaks prisoner and insisted that they guide him to the source of the gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-52">[49]</a></sup> From the entry in <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus%27s_journal" title="Christopher Columbus's journal">his journal</a> of 12 October 1492, in which he wrote of them: "Many of the men I have seen have scars on their bodies, and when I made signs to them to find out how this happened, they indicated that people from other nearby islands come to San Salvador to capture them; they defend themselves the best they can. I believe that people from the mainland come here to take them as slaves. They ought to make good and skilled servants, for they repeat very quickly whatever we say to them. I think they can very easily be made Christians, for they seem to have no religion. If it pleases our Lord, I will take six of them to Your Highnesses when I depart, in order that they may learn our language."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> Columbus remarked that their lack of modern weaponry and metal-forged swords or pikes was a tactical vulnerability, writing, "I could conquer the whole of them with 50 men, and govern them as I pleased."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus also explored the northeast coast of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, where he landed on 28 October. On 22 November, Martín Alonso Pinzón took the <i>Pinta</i> on an unauthorized expedition in search of an island called "Babeque" or "Baneque", which the natives had told him was rich in gold. Columbus, for his part, continued to the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, where he landed on 5 December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering-1st_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pickering-1st-55">[52]</a></sup> There, the <i>Santa María</i> ran aground on Christmas Day 1492 and had to be abandoned. The wreck was used as a target for cannon fire to impress the native peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> Columbus was received by the native <a href="/wiki/Cacique" title="Cacique">cacique</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guacanagari" title="Guacanagari">Guacanagari</a>, who gave him permission to leave some of his men behind. Columbus left 39 men, including <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Torres" title="Luis de Torres">Luis de Torres</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Converso" title="Converso">Converso</a> interpreter, who spoke <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and founded the settlement of <i><a href="/wiki/La_Navidad" title="La Navidad">La Navidad</a></i> at the site of present-day Bord de Mer de Limonade, <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> Columbus took more natives prisoner and continued his exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-52">[49]</a></sup> He kept sailing along the northern coast of Hispaniola with a single ship, until he encountered Pinzón and the <i>Pinta</i> on 6 January.</p>
<p>On 13 January 1493, Columbus made his last stop of this voyage in the New World, in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Rinc%C3%B3n" title="Bay of Rincón">Bay of Rincón</a> at the eastern end of the <a href="/wiki/Saman%C3%A1_Peninsula" title="Samaná Peninsula">Samaná Peninsula</a> in northeast Hispaniola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> There he encountered the warlike <a href="/wiki/Ciguayos" title="Ciguayos">Cigüayos</a>, the only natives who offered violent resistance during his first voyage to the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> The Cigüayos refused to trade the amount of bows and arrows that Columbus desired; in the ensuing clash one Ciguayo was stabbed in the buttocks and another wounded with an arrow in his chest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> Because of this and because of the Cigüayos' use of arrows, he called the inlet where he met them <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Arrows" title="Bay of Arrows">the Bay of Arrows (or Gulf of Arrows)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> Columbus kidnapped about 10 to 25 natives and took them back with him (only seven or eight of the natives arrived in Spain alive).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewen1995_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewen1995-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WC_Delacroix,Eugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="888" data-file-width="1168" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg/250px-WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg/375px-WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg/500px-WC_Delacroix%2CEugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WC_Delacroix,Eugene_The_Return_of_Christopher_Columbus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The return of Christopher Columbus; his audience before King Ferdinand and Queen Isabella</i>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Columbus headed for Spain on the <i>Niña</i>, but a storm separated him from the <i>Pinta,</i> and forced the <i>Niña</i> to stop at the island of Santa Maria in the Azores. Half of his crew went ashore to say prayers in a chapel to give thanks for having survived the storm. But while praying, they were imprisoned by the governor of the island, ostensibly on suspicion of being pirates. After a two-day standoff, the prisoners were released, and Columbus again set sail for Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another storm forced him into the port at <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> He anchored next to the King's harbor patrol ship on 4 March 1493 in Portugal. There, he was interviewed by <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">Bartolomeu Dias</a>, who had rounded the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> a few years earlier, in 1488–1489. Dias's success had complicated Columbus's attempts to secure funding from the Portuguese court because the sure route to the Indies that Dias pioneered made a risky, conjectural western route unnecessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> Not finding King <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">John II of Portugal</a> in Lisbon, Columbus wrote a letter to him and waited for John's reply. John asked Columbus to go to <i>Vale do Paraíso</i> north of Lisbon to meet him. Relations between Portugal and Castile were poor at the time. Columbus went to meet with John at <i>Vale do Paraíso</i>. Hearing of Columbus's discoveries, John told him that he believed the voyage to be in violation of the 1479 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alc%C3%A1%C3%A7ovas" title="Treaty of Alcáçovas">Treaty of Alcáçovas</a>.</p>
<p>After spending more than a week in Portugal, and paying his respects to <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Viseu" title="Eleanor of Viseu">Eleanor of Viseu</a>, Columbus again set sail for Spain. <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Ferdinand Magellan</a> was a young boy and a ward of Eleanor's court; it is likely he saw Columbus during this visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-32">[30]</a></sup> After departing, and after reportedly being saved from assassins by King John, Columbus crossed the bar of Saltes and entered the harbor of Palos de la Frontera on 15 March 1493. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus_Letter_on_the_First_Voyage" title="Columbus Letter on the First Voyage">Word of his finding new lands</a> rapidly spread throughout Europe.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_voyage">Second voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_second_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="1001" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Columbus_second_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_second_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_second_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus's second voyage</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Columbus left the port of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a> on 24 September 1493, with a fleet of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_second_voyage" title="Columbus's second voyage">17 ships</a> carrying 1,200 men and the supplies to establish permanent colonies in the New World. The passengers included priests, farmers, and soldiers, who would be the new colonists. This reflected the new policy of creating not just "colonies of exploitation", but also "colonies of settlement" from which to launch missions dedicated to converting the natives to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> Modern studies suggest that, as reported by the <i>Washington Post</i>, "crew members may have included free black Africans who arrived in the New World about a decade before the slave trade began."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>As in the first voyage, the fleet stopped at the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, from which it departed on 13 October, following a more southerly course than on the previous expedition. On 3 November, Columbus sighted a rugged island that he named <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">Dominica</a> (Latin for Sunday); later that day, he landed at <a href="/wiki/Marie-Galante" title="Marie-Galante">Marie-Galante</a>, which he named <i>Santa María la Galante</i>. After sailing past <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Les_Saintes" title="Les Saintes">Les Saintes</a> (<i>Los Santos</i>, "The Saints"), he arrived at the island of <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a>, which he named <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_de_Guadalupe" title="Santa María de Guadalupe">Santa María de Guadalupe</a> de Extremadura</i>, after the image of the Virgin Mary venerated at the Spanish monastery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Villuercas" title="Villuercas">Villuercas</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Guadalupe,_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Guadalupe, Cáceres">Guadalupe, Cáceres</a>, Spain. He explored that island from 4 to 10 November.</p>
<p>Michele da Cuneo, Columbus's childhood friend from <a href="/wiki/Savona" title="Savona">Savona</a>, sailed with Columbus during the second voyage and wrote: "In my opinion, since Genoa was Genoa, there was never born a man so well equipped and expert in the art of navigation as the said lord Admiral."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> Columbus named the small island of "Saona ... to honor Michele da Cuneo, his friend from Savona."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same childhood friend reported in a letter that Columbus had provided one of the captured indigenous women to him. He wrote, "While I was in the boat, I captured a very beautiful Carib woman, whom the said Lord Admiral gave to me. When I had taken her to my cabin she was naked—as was their custom. I was filled with a desire to take my pleasure with her and attempted to satisfy my desire. She was unwilling, and so treated me with her nails that I wished I had never begun. But—to cut a long story short—I then took a piece of rope and whipped her soundly, and she let forth such incredible screams that you would not have believed your ears. Eventually we came to such terms, I assure you, that you would have thought that she had been brought up in a school for whores."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon,_1856.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3011" data-file-width="2074" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg/220px-Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg/330px-Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg/440px-Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon%2C_1856.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inspiracion_de_Cristobal_Colon_by_Jose_Maria_Obregon,_1856.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Inspiration of Christopher Columbus</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez" title="José María Velasco Gómez">José María Obregón</a>, 1856</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pedro de las Casas, father of the priest <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">Bartolomé de las Casas</a>, also accompanied Columbus on this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-traboulay_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-traboulay-68">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exact course of Columbus's voyage through the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a> is debated, but it seems likely that he turned north, sighting and naming several islands, including:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Montserrat" title="Montserrat">Montserrat</a> (for Santa María de Montserrate, after the Blessed Virgin of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastery_of_Montserrat" title="Monastery of Montserrat">Monastery of Montserrat</a>, which is located on the Mountain of Montserrat, in Catalonia, Spain),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a> (after a church in <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a>, Spain, called Santa María la Antigua, meaning "Old St. Mary's"),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Redonda" title="Redonda">Redonda</a> (<i>Santa María la Redonda</i>, Spanish for "St. Mary the Round", owing to the island's shape),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nevis" title="Nevis">Nevis</a> (derived from the Spanish <i>Nuestra Señora de las Nieves</i>, "Our Lady of the Snows", because Columbus thought the clouds over Nevis Peak made the island resemble a snow-capped mountain),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saint_Kitts" title="Saint Kitts">Saint Kitts</a> (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Christopher" title="St. Christopher">St. Christopher</a>, patron of sailors and travelers),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sint_Eustatius" title="Sint Eustatius">Sint Eustatius</a> (for the early Roman martyr, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Eustace" title="Saint Eustace">St. Eustachius</a>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saba" title="Saba">Saba</a> (after the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Queen_of_Sheba" title="Queen of Sheba">Queen of Sheba</a>),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saint_Martin" title="Saint Martin">Saint Martin</a> (<i>San Martín</i>), and</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Croix" title="Saint Croix">Saint Croix</a> (from the Spanish <i>Santa Cruz</i>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">Holy Cross</a>").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Columbus also sighted the chain of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Islands" title="Virgin Islands">Virgin Islands</a>, which he named <i>Islas de Santa Úrsula y las Once Mil Vírgenes</i>, "Islands of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Ursula" title="Saint Ursula">Saint Ursula</a> and the 11,000 Virgins" (shortened, both on maps of the time and in common parlance, to <i>Islas Vírgenes</i>). He also named the islands of <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Gorda" title="Virgin Gorda">Virgin Gorda</a> ("Fat Virgin"), <a href="/wiki/Tortola" title="Tortola">Tortola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Island" title="Peter Island">Peter Island</a> (<i>San Pedro</i>).</p>
<p>He continued to the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Antilles" title="Greater Antilles">Greater Antilles</a>, and landed in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, which he named <i>San Juan Bautista</i> in honor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_John_the_Baptist" title="Saint John the Baptist">Saint John the Baptist</a> (a name that was later retained only for the capital city of <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a>). One of the first skirmishes between <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> and Europeans since the time of the Vikings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992-69">[66]</a></sup> occurred when Columbus's men rescued two native boys who had just been castrated by their captors in another tribe.</p>
<p>On 22 November, Columbus returned to Hispaniola, where he intended to visit the fort of <a href="/wiki/La_Navidad" title="La Navidad">La Navidad</a>, built during his first voyage and located on the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>. Columbus found the fort in ruins, destroyed by the native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%C3%ADno_people" title="Taíno people">Taino</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> Among the ruins were the corpses of 11 of the 39 Spaniards who had stayed behind as the first colonists in the New World.</p>
<p>Columbus then sailed more than 100 kilometres (62 miles) eastwards along the northern coast of Hispaniola, establishing a new settlement, which he called <a href="/wiki/La_Isabela" title="La Isabela">La Isabela</a>, in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/La_Isabela" title="La Isabela">La Isabela</a> proved to be poorly located and the settlement was short-lived.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_voyage">Third voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_third_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="638" data-file-width="901" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Columbus_third_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_third_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_third_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Third voyage</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the abstract of Columbus's journal made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_Las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de Las Casas">Bartolomé de Las Casas</a>, the objective of the third voyage was to verify the existence of a continent that King John II of Portugal suggested was located to the southwest of the Cape Verde Islands. King John reportedly knew of the existence of such a mainland because "canoes had been found which set out from the coast of Guinea [West Africa] and sailed to the west with merchandise."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 30 May 1498, Columbus left with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_third_voyage" title="Columbus's third voyage">six ships</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sanl%C3%BAcar_de_Barrameda" title="Sanlúcar de Barrameda">Sanlúcar, Spain</a>, for his third trip to the New World. Three of the ships headed directly for Hispaniola with much-needed supplies, while Columbus took the other three in an exploration of what might lie to the south of the Caribbean islands he had already visited, including a hoped-for passage to continental Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minster-3rd_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minster-3rd-74">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus led his fleet to the Portuguese island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porto_Santo" title="Porto Santo">Porto Santo</a>, his wife's native land. He then sailed to <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and spent some time there with the Portuguese captain João Gonçalves da Camara, before sailing to the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a>. As he crossed the Atlantic, Columbus discovered that the angle between North as indicated by a magnetic compass and North as measured by the position of the <a href="/wiki/Pole_star" title="Pole star">pole star</a> changed with his position (a phenomenon now known as "<a href="/wiki/Magnetic_declination" title="Magnetic declination">compass variation</a>"). He would later use his previous measurements of the compass variation to adjust his reckoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-online_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB-online-7">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>After being becalmed for several days in the <a href="/wiki/Doldrums" title="Doldrums">doldrums</a> of the mid-Atlantic, Columbus's fleet regained its wind and, dangerously low on water, turned north in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">Dominica</a>, which Columbus had visited in his previous voyage. The ships arrived at King John's hypothesized continent, which is <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, when they sighted the land of <a href="/wiki/Trinidad" title="Trinidad">Trinidad</a> on 31 July approaching from the southeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> The fleet sailed along the southern coast and entered <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Channel" title="Columbus Channel">Dragon's Mouth</a>, anchoring near <a href="/wiki/Soldado_Rock" title="Soldado Rock">Soldado Rock</a> where they made contact with a group of native <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Amerindians</a> in canoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> Columbus then landed on Trinidad at <a href="/wiki/Icacos_Point" title="Icacos Point">Icacos Point</a> (which he named <i>Punta de Arenal</i>) on 2 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> After resupplying with food and water, from 4 to 12 August Columbus explored the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Paria" title="Gulf of Paria">Gulf of Paria</a>, which separates Trinidad from what is now <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, near the delta of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orinoco_River" title="Orinoco River">Orinoco River</a>. He then touched the mainland of South America at the <a href="/wiki/Paria_Peninsula" title="Paria Peninsula">Paria Peninsula</a>.</p>
<p>Exploring the new continent, Columbus correctly interpreted the enormous quantity of fresh water that the Orinoco delivered into the Atlantic Ocean as evidence that he had reached a large landmass rather than another island. As he sailed the Gulf of Paria, he observed the <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_motion" title="Diurnal motion">diurnal rotation</a> of the pole star in the sky, which he erroneously interpreted as evidence that the Earth was not perfectly spherical, but rather bulged out like a pear around the new-found continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-online_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-EB-online-7">[6]</a></sup> He also speculated that the new continent might be the location of the biblical <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a>. He then sailed to the islands of <a href="/wiki/Chacachacare" title="Chacachacare">Chacachacare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margarita_Island" title="Margarita Island">Margarita</a>. He sighted <a href="/wiki/Tobago" title="Tobago">Tobago</a> (which he named "Bella Forma") and <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a> (which he named "Concepción").</p>
<p>In poor health, Columbus returned to <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> on 19 August, only to find that many of the Spanish settlers of the new colony were in rebellion against his rule, claiming that Columbus had misled them about the supposedly bountiful riches of the New World. A number of returning settlers and sailors lobbied against Columbus at the <a href="/wiki/Cortes_Generales" title="Cortes Generales">Spanish court</a>, accusing him and his brothers of gross mismanagement. Columbus had some of his crew hanged for disobedience. He had an economic interest in the enslavement of the Hispaniola natives and for that reason was not eager to baptize them, which attracted criticism from some churchmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varela_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-varela-78">[75]</a></sup> An entry in his journal from September 1498 reads: "From here one might send, in the name of the Holy Trinity, as many slaves as could be sold ..."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
