<p>Columbus was eventually forced to make peace with the rebellious colonists on humiliating terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering-3rd_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pickering-3rd-79">[76]</a></sup> In 1500, the Crown had him removed as governor, arrested, and transported in chains to Spain (see <a href="#Accusations_of_tyranny_during_governorship">"Accusations of tyranny during governorship"</a> section below). He was eventually freed and allowed to return to the New World, but not as governor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_voyage">Fourth voyage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="1001" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/350px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/525px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg/700px-Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_fourth_voyage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus's fourth voyage</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Colon_COA_(2).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="550" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/200px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/300px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg/400px-House_of_Colon_COA_%282%29.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Colon_COA_(2).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coat_of_Arms" title="Coat of Arms">Coat of Arms</a> granted to Christopher Columbus and the <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Col%C3%B3n_(familia)" title="es:Colón (familia)">House of Colon</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Motu_proprio" title="Motu proprio">motu proprio</a></i> in 1502.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before leaving for his fourth voyage, Columbus wrote a letter to the Governors of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Saint_George" title="Bank of Saint George">Bank of Saint George</a>, Genoa, dated at Seville, 2 April 1502.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> He wrote "Although my body is here my heart is always near you."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus made a fourth voyage nominally in search of the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> to the Indian Ocean. Accompanied by his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bartolomeo_Columbus" title="Bartolomeo Columbus">Bartolomeo</a> and his 13-year-old son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fernando_Col%C3%B3n" title="Fernando Colón">Fernando</a>, he left <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a> on 11 May 1502, with his flagship <i>Santa María</i> and the vessels <i>Gallega</i>, <i>Vizcaína</i>, and <i>Santiago de Palos</i>. He sailed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arzila" title="Arzila">Arzila</a> on the Moroccan coast to rescue Portuguese soldiers whom he had heard were under siege by the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>.</p>
<p>On 15 June, they landed at Carbet on the island of <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a> (<i>Martinica</i>). A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a> was brewing, so he continued on, hoping to find shelter on <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>. He arrived at <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a> on 29 June, but was denied port, and the new governor refused to listen to his storm prediction. Instead, while Columbus's ships sheltered at the mouth of the Rio Jaina, the first <a href="/wiki/Spanish_treasure_fleet" title="Spanish treasure fleet">Spanish treasure fleet</a> sailed into the hurricane. Columbus's ships survived with only minor damage, while 29 of the 30 ships in the governor's fleet were lost to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1492-1524_Atlantic_hurricane_seasons" title="1492-1524 Atlantic hurricane seasons">a storm on 1 July</a>. In addition to the ships, 500 lives (including that of the governor, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Bobadilla" title="Francisco de Bobadilla">Francisco de Bobadilla</a>) and an immense cargo of gold were surrendered to the sea.</p>
<p>After a brief stop at <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, Columbus sailed to Central America, arriving at <a href="/wiki/Guanaja" title="Guanaja">Guanaja</a> (Isla de Pinos) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islas_de_la_Bah%C3%ADa_department" title="Islas de la Bahía department">Bay Islands</a> off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> on 30 July. Here Bartolomeo found native merchants and a large canoe, which was described as being "long as a galley" and filled with cargo. On 14 August, he landed on the continental mainland at <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Castilla,_Honduras" title="Puerto Castilla, Honduras">Puerto Castilla</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Trujillo,_Honduras" title="Trujillo, Honduras">Trujillo, Honduras</a>. He spent two months exploring the coasts of Honduras, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, before arriving in <a href="/wiki/Almirante,_Bocas_del_Toro" title="Almirante, Bocas del Toro">Almirante</a> Bay in <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> on 16 October.</p>
<p>On 5 December 1502, Columbus and his crew found themselves in a storm unlike any they had ever experienced. In his journal Columbus writes,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For nine days I was as one lost, without hope of life. Eyes never beheld the sea so angry, so high, so covered with foam. The wind not only prevented our progress, but offered no opportunity to run behind any headland for shelter; hence we were forced to keep out in this bloody ocean, seething like a pot on a hot fire. Never did the sky look more terrible; for one whole day and night it blazed like a furnace, and the lightning broke with such violence that each time I wondered if it had carried off my spars and sails; the flashes came with such fury and frightfulness that we all thought that the ship would be blasted. All this time the water never ceased to fall from the sky; I do not say it rained, for it was like another deluge. The men were so worn out that they longed for death to end their dreadful suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1302" data-file-width="1602" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg/250px-Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg/375px-Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg/500px-Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eclipse_Christophe_Colomb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus awes the Jamaican natives by predicting the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse</a> of 1504.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Panama, Columbus learned from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ngobe" title="Ngobe">Ngobe</a> of gold and a strait to another ocean, but was told by local leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quib%C3%ADan" title="Quibían">Quibían</a> not to go past a certain point down the river. After much exploration, in January 1503, he established a <a href="/wiki/Garrison" title="Garrison">garrison</a> at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Bel%C3%A9n_River" title="Belén River">Belén River</a>. On 6 April, one of the ships became stranded in the river. At the same time, the garrison was attacked by Quibían and the other ships were damaged. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipworm" title="Shipworm">Shipworms</a> also damaged the ships in tropical waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Columbus left for Hispaniola on 16 April heading north. On 10 May he sighted the <a href="/wiki/Cayman_Islands" title="Cayman Islands">Cayman Islands</a>, naming them "<i>Las Tortugas</i>" after the numerous <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> there. His ships next sustained more damage in a storm off the coast of Cuba. Unable to travel farther, on 25 June 1503 they were beached in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Ann_Parish,_Jamaica" title="Saint Ann Parish, Jamaica">St. Ann's Bay, Jamaica</a>.</p>
<p>For one year Columbus and his men remained stranded on <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>. A Spaniard, Diego Méndez, and some natives paddled a <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">canoe</a> to get help from <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>. The governor, <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_de_Ovando_y_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres">Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres</a>, detested Columbus and obstructed all efforts to rescue him and his men. In the meantime Columbus, in a desperate effort to induce the natives to continue provisioning him and his hungry men, won their favor by predicting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/February_1504_lunar_eclipse" title="February 1504 lunar eclipse">lunar eclipse for 29 February 1504</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">Abraham Zacuto</a>'s astronomical charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> Help finally arrived, no thanks to the governor, on 29 June 1504, and Columbus and his men arrived in <a href="/wiki/Sanl%C3%BAcar_de_Barrameda" title="Sanlúcar de Barrameda">Sanlúcar, Spain</a>, on 7 November.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_tyranny"><span id="Accusations_of_tyranny_during_governorship"></span> Accusations of tyranny</span></h2>
<p>Following his first voyage, Columbus was appointed Viceroy and Governor of the Indies under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capitulations of Santa Fe">Capitulations of Santa Fe</a>. In practice, this primarily entailed the administration of the colonies in the island of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, whose capital was established in <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a>. By the end of his third voyage, Columbus was physically and mentally exhausted, his body wracked by <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> and his eyes by <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmia" title="Ophthalmia">ophthalmia</a>. In October 1499, he sent two ships to Spain, asking the Court of Spain to appoint a royal commissioner to help him govern.</p>
<p>By this time, accusations of tyranny and incompetence on the part of Columbus had also reached the Court. Queen Isabella and King Ferdinand responded by removing Columbus from power and replacing him with <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Bobadilla" title="Francisco de Bobadilla">Francisco de Bobadilla</a>, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Calatrava" title="Order of Calatrava">Order of Calatrava</a>. Bobadilla, who ruled as governor from 1500 until his death in a storm in 1502, had also been tasked by the Court with investigating the accusations of brutality made against Columbus. Arriving in Santo Domingo while Columbus was away in the explorations of his <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus#Third_voyage" title="Christopher Columbus">third voyage</a>, Bobadilla was immediately met with complaints about all three Columbus brothers: Christopher, Bartolomeo, and Diego. Bobadilla reported to Spain that Columbus regularly used <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">mutilation</a> to govern Hispaniola. The 48-page report, found in 2006 in the national archive in the Spanish city of <a href="/wiki/Simancas" title="Simancas">Simancas</a>, contains testimonies from 23 people, including both enemies and supporters of Columbus, about the treatment of colonial subjects by Columbus and his brothers during his seven-year rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper1_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper1-87">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the report, Columbus once punished a man found guilty of stealing corn by having his ears and nose cut off and then selling him into slavery. Testimony recorded in the report stated that Columbus congratulated his brother Bartolomeo on "defending the family" when the latter ordered a woman paraded naked through the streets and then had her tongue cut out for suggesting that Columbus was of lowly birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper1_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper1-87">[84]</a></sup> The document also describes how Columbus put down native unrest and revolt; he first ordered a brutal crackdown in which many natives were killed and then paraded their <a href="/wiki/Dismemberment" title="Dismemberment">dismembered</a> bodies through the streets in an attempt to discourage further rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A.26E_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-A.26E-88">[85]</a></sup> "Columbus's government was characterised by a form of tyranny," Consuelo Varela, a Spanish historian who has seen the document, told journalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper1_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper1-87">[84]</a></sup> "Even those who loved him had to admit the atrocities that had taken place."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newspaper1_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-newspaper1-87">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of their gross misgovernance, Columbus and his brothers were arrested and imprisoned upon their return to Spain from the third voyage. They lingered in jail for six weeks before King Ferdinand ordered their release. Not long after, the king and queen summoned the Columbus brothers to the <a href="/wiki/Alhambra" title="Alhambra">Alhambra</a> palace in <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a>. There, the royal couple heard the brothers' pleas; restored their freedom and wealth; and, after much persuasion, agreed to fund Columbus's fourth voyage. But the door was firmly shut on Columbus's role as governor. Henceforth <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_de_Ovando_y_C%C3%A1ceres" title="Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres">Nicolás de Ovando y Cáceres</a> was to be the new governor of the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg/170px-ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg/255px-ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg/340px-ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColumbusHouseOfValladolid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replica of the <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa María (ship)">Santa María</a></i>, Columbus's flagship during his first voyage, at his <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a> house<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Columbus had always claimed the conversion of non-believers as one reason for his explorations, but he grew increasingly religious in his later years. Probably with the assistance of his son Diego and his friend the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthusian" title="Carthusian">Carthusian</a> monk Gaspar Gorricio, Columbus produced two books during his later years: a <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Privileges" title="Book of Privileges">Book of Privileges</a></i> (1502), detailing and documenting the rewards from the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Spain" title="Monarchy of Spain">Spanish Crown</a> to which he believed he and his heirs were entitled, and a <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Prophecies" title="Book of Prophecies">Book of Prophecies</a></i> (1505), in which he considered his achievements as an explorer but a fulfillment of Bible prophecy in the context of <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFroom19502_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFroom19502-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his later years, Columbus demanded that the Spanish Crown give him 10 percent of all profits made in the new lands, as stipulated in the <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_Santa_Fe" title="Capitulations of Santa Fe">Capitulations of Santa Fe</a>. Because he had been relieved of his duties as governor, the crown did not feel bound by that contract and his demands were rejected. After his death, his heirs sued the Crown for a part of the profits from trade with America, as well as other rewards. This led to a protracted series of legal disputes known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pleitos_colombinos" title="Pleitos colombinos">pleitos colombinos</a></i> ("Columbian lawsuits").</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Illness_and_death">Illness and death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_death_of_Columbus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="640" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/The_death_of_Columbus.jpg/170px-The_death_of_Columbus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/The_death_of_Columbus.jpg/255px-The_death_of_Columbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/The_death_of_Columbus.jpg/340px-The_death_of_Columbus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_death_of_Columbus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The death of Columbus</i>, lithograph by L. Prang &amp; Co., 1893</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During a violent storm on his first return voyage, Columbus, then 41, suffered an attack of what was believed at the time to be <a href="/wiki/Gout" title="Gout">gout</a>. In subsequent years, he was plagued with what was thought to be <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> and other fevers, bleeding from the eyes, and prolonged attacks of gout. The suspected attacks increased in duration and severity, sometimes leaving Columbus bedridden for months at a time, and culminated in his death 14 years later.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="1627" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/170px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/255px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg/340px-Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tumba_de_Colon-Sevilla.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tomb in <a href="/wiki/Seville_Cathedral" title="Seville Cathedral">Seville Cathedral</a>. The remains are borne by kings of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Kingdom of León">Leon</a>, Aragon and <a href="/wiki/Navarre" title="Navarre">Navarre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Based on Columbus's lifestyle and the described symptoms, modern doctors suspect that he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Reactive_arthritis" title="Reactive arthritis">Reiter's syndrome</a>, rather than gout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMD_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-UMD-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoenig_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoenig-93">[90]</a></sup> Reiter's syndrome is a common presentation of reactive arthritis, a joint inflammation caused by intestinal bacterial infections or after acquiring certain sexually transmitted diseases (primarily chlamydia or <a href="/wiki/Gonorrhea" title="Gonorrhea">gonorrhea</a>). "It seems likely that [Columbus] acquired reactive arthritis from food poisoning on one of his ocean voyages because of poor sanitation and improper food preparation," writes Dr. Frank C. Arnett, a <a href="/wiki/Rheumatology" title="Rheumatology">rheumatologist</a> and professor of internal medicine, pathology and laboratory medicine the University of Texas Medical School at Houston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMD_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-UMD-92">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 20 May 1506, aged probably 54, Columbus died in <a href="/wiki/Valladolid" title="Valladolid">Valladolid</a>, Spain. His remains were first interred at Valladolid, then at the monastery of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Cartuja" title="La Cartuja">La Cartuja</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> (southern Spain) by the will of his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diego_Col%C3%B3n" title="Diego Colón">Diego Colón</a>, who had been governor of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>. In 1542, the remains were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Ciudad_Colonial_(Santo_Domingo)" title="Ciudad Colonial (Santo Domingo)">Colonial Santo Domingo</a>, in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>. In 1795, when France took over the entire island of <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>, Columbus's remains were moved to <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, Cuba. After Cuba became independent following the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish–American War">Spanish–American War</a> in 1898, the remains were moved back to Spain, to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Seville" title="Cathedral of Seville">Cathedral of Seville</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> where they were placed on an elaborate <a href="/wiki/Catafalque" title="Catafalque">catafalque</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="500" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png/220px-Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png/330px-Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png/440px-Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Caravel._Ashes_of_Christopher_Columbus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver Caravel. Ashes of Christopher Columbus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, a lead box bearing an inscription identifying "Don Christopher Columbus" and containing bone fragments and a bullet was discovered at <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a> in 1877. To lay to rest claims that the wrong relics had been moved to Havana and that Columbus's remains had been left buried in the cathedral at <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> samples of the corpse resting in <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> were taken in June 2003 (<i>History Today</i> August 2003) as well as other DNA samples from the remains of his brother Diego and younger son <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Columbus" title="Ferdinand Columbus">Fernando Colón</a>. Initial observations suggested that the bones did not appear to belong to somebody with the physique or age at death associated with Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> DNA extraction proved difficult; only short fragments of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> could be isolated. The mitochondrial DNA fragments matched corresponding DNA from Columbus's brother, giving support that both individuals had shared the same mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-97">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Such evidence, together with <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologic</a> and historic analyses, led the researchers to conclude that the remains found in Seville belonged to Christopher Columbus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bare_url_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url-98">[95]</a></sup> The authorities in Santo Domingo have never allowed the remains there to be exhumed, so it is unknown if any of those remains could be from Columbus's body as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bare_url_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url-98">[95]</a></sup> The Dominican remains are located in "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Columbus_Lighthouse" title="The Columbus Lighthouse">The Columbus Lighthouse</a>" (<i>Faro a Colón</i>), in Santo Domingo.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2>
<p>The anniversary of Columbus's 1492 landing in the Americas is usually observed on 12 October in Spain and throughout the Americas, except Canada. In Spain it is called the <i>Fiesta Nacional de España y Día de la Hispanidad</i>, while a number of countries in Latin America celebrate it as <i>Día de la Raza</i>. In the United States it is called <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Day" title="Columbus Day">Columbus Day</a> and is observed annually on the second Monday in October.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_1892_Issue-$5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1044" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg/220px-Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg/330px-Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg/440px-Columbus_1892_Issue-%245.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbus_1892_Issue-$5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Issue" title="Columbian Issue">Columbian Issue</a> of 1893.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Columbian_Exposition" title="World Columbian Exposition">World Columbian Exposition</a> in Chicago, 1893, commemorated the 400th anniversary of the landing of Christopher Columbus in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-99">[96]</a></sup> Over 27 million people attended the exposition during its six-month duration.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> participated in the celebration issuing the first US <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_stamp" title="Commemorative stamp"><i>commemorative postage stamps</i></a>, a series of 16 postage issues called the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Issue" title="Columbian Issue">Columbian Issue</a> depicting Columbus, Queen Isabella and others in the various stages of his several voyages. The issues range in value from the 1-cent to the 5-dollar denominations. Under Benjamin Harrison and his Postmaster General <a href="/wiki/John_Wanamaker" title="John Wanamaker">John Wanamaker</a> the Columbian commemorative stamps were made available and were first issued at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Columbian_Exposition" title="World Columbian Exposition">World Columbian Exposition</a> in Chicago, Illinois, in 1893. Wanamaker originally introduced the idea of issuing the nation's first commemorative stamp to Harrison, the Congress and the U.S. Post Office. To demonstrate his confidence in the new Columbian commemorative issues Wanamaker purchased $10,000 worth of stamps with his own money. The Columbian Exposition lasted several months, and over $40 million in commemorative postage stamps had been sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JOHN_WANAMAKER_PMG_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-JOHN_WANAMAKER_PMG-100">[97]</a></sup> The 400th anniversary Columbian issues were very popular in the United States. A total of two billion stamps were issued for all the Columbian denominations, and 72 percent of these were the two-cent stamps, "Landing of Columbus", which paid the first-class rate for domestic mail at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992, a second Columbian issue was released that was identical to the first to commemorate the 500th anniversary, except for the date in the upper right hand corner of each stamp. These issues were made from the original dies of which the first engraved issues of 1893 were produced. The United States issued the series jointly for the first time with three other countries, Italy in lire, Portugal in escudos and Spain in pesetas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoverer">Discoverer</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faro_colon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/220px-Faro_colon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/330px-Faro_colon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Faro_colon.jpg/440px-Faro_colon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faro_colon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus Lighthouse (<i>Faro a Colón</i>), <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though Christopher Columbus came to be considered the "discoverer of America" in US and European popular culture, his true historical legacy is more nuanced. America was first discovered by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">its indigenous population</a>, and Columbus was not even the first European to reach its shores, as he was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> at <a href="/wiki/L%27Anse_aux_Meadows" title="L'Anse aux Meadows">L'Anse aux Meadows</a>. But the lasting significance of Columbus's voyages outshone that of his Viking predecessors, because he managed to bring word of the continent back to Europe. By bringing the continent to the forefront of Western attention, Columbus initiated the enduring relationship between the Earth's two major landmasses and their inhabitants. "Columbus's claim to fame isn't that he got there first," explains historian Martin Dugard, "it's that he stayed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dugard_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-dugard-104">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians have traditionally argued that Columbus remained convinced to the very end that his journeys had been along the east coast of Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> but writer <a href="/wiki/Kirkpatrick_Sale" title="Kirkpatrick Sale">Kirkpatrick Sale</a> argues that a document in the <i>Book of Privileges</i> indicates Columbus knew he found a new continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> Furthermore, his journals from the third voyage call the "land of Paria" a "hitherto unknown" continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeruvabel2003_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeruvabel2003-107">[104]</a></sup> On the other hand, his other writings continued to claim that he had reached Asia, such as a 1502 letter to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">Pope Alexander VI</a> where he asserted that Cuba was the east coast of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992227_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillips.2C_Jr1992227-108">[105]</a></sup> He also rationalized that the new continent of South America was the "Earthly Paradise" that was located "at the end of the Orient".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeruvabel2003_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeruvabel2003-107">[104]</a></sup> Thus, it remains unclear what his true beliefs were.</p>
<p>The term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a>" is usually used to refer to the peoples and cultures of the Americas before the arrival of Columbus and his European successors.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flat_Earth_mythology">Flat Earth mythology</span></h3>
<p>Columbus is often credited with refuting a prevalent belief in a flat Earth. However, this legacy is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_common_misconceptions" title="List of common misconceptions">popular misconception</a>. To the contrary, the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_Earth" title="Spherical Earth">spherical shape of the Earth</a> had been known to scholars since <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">antiquity</a>, and was common knowledge among sailors. Coincidentally, the oldest surviving globe of the Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Erdapfel" title="Erdapfel">Erdapfel</a>, was made in 1492 just before Columbus's return to Europe. As such it contains no sign of the Americas and yet demonstrates the common belief in a spherical Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="America_as_a_distinct_land">America as a distinct land</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1944" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/220px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/330px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg/440px-1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1893_Nina_Pinta_Santa_Maria_replicas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replicas of <i>Niña</i>, <i>Pinta</i> and <i>Santa Maria</i> sailed from Spain to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Columbian_Exposition" title="Chicago Columbian Exposition">Chicago Columbian Exposition</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_monument,_Denver,_CO_IMG_5575.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG/220px-Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG/330px-Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG/440px-Christopher_Columbus_monument%2C_Denver%2C_CO_IMG_5575.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_monument,_Denver,_CO_IMG_5575.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Columbus monument near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_capitol" title="State capitol">state capitol</a> in <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, Colorado<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The scholar <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a>, who sailed to America in the years following Columbus's first voyage, was the first to speculate that the land was not part of Asia but in fact constituted some wholly new continent previously unknown to Eurasians. His travel journals, published 1502–04, convinced German cartographer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a> to reach the same conclusion, and in 1507—a year after Columbus's death—Waldseemüller published a world map <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/America_(name)" title="America (name)">calling the new continent</a> <i>America</i> from Vespucci's Latinized name "Americus". According to Paul Lunde, "The preoccupation of European courts with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a> in the East partly explains their relative lack of interest in Columbus's discoveries in the West."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically, the English had downplayed Columbus and emphasized the role of the Venetian <a href="/wiki/John_Cabot" title="John Cabot">John Cabot</a> as a pioneer explorer, but for the emerging United States, Cabot made for a poor national hero. Veneration of Columbus in America dates back to colonial times. The name <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">Columbia</a> for "America" first appeared in a 1738 weekly publication of the debates of the British Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup> The use of Columbus as a founding figure of New World nations and the use of the word "Columbia", or simply the name "Columbus", spread rapidly after the American Revolution. Columbus's name was given to the federal capital of the United States (<a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital cities</a> of two U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_(United_States)" title="State (United States)">states</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_River" title="Columbia River">Columbia River</a>. Outside the United States the name was used in 1819 for the <a href="/wiki/Gran_Colombia" title="Gran Colombia">Gran Colombia</a>, a precursor of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Colombia" title="Republic of Colombia">Republic of Colombia</a>. Numerous cities, towns, counties, streets, and plazas (called Plaza Colón or Plaza de Colón throughout Latin America and Spain) have been named after him. A candidate for sainthood in the Catholic Church in 1866, celebration of Columbus's legacy perhaps reached a zenith in 1892 with the 400th anniversary of his first arrival in the Americas. Monuments to Columbus like the <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World's Columbian Exposition">Columbian Exposition</a> in Chicago and <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Circle" title="Columbus Circle">Columbus Circle</a> in New York City were erected throughout the United States and Latin America extolling him.</p>
<p>In 1909, descendants of Columbus undertook to dismantle the Columbus family chapel in Spain and move it to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boalsburg" title="Boalsburg">Boalsburg</a> near <a href="/wiki/State_College,_Pennsylvania" title="State College, Pennsylvania">State College</a>, Pennsylvania, where it may now be visited by the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> At the museum associated with the chapel, there are a number of Columbus relics worthy of note, including the armchair that the "Admiral of the Ocean Sea" used at his chart table.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_in_modern_scholarship">Criticism in modern scholarship</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="400" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" width="45"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this section is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Christopher_Columbus#.23" title="Talk:Christopher Columbus">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span> <small><i>(July 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>More recent views of Columbus have been critical of his colonization and treatment of natives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup> Among reasons for this criticism is the treatment and disappearance of the native Taíno people of Hispaniola, where Columbus began a rudimentary tribute system of gold and cotton. The people disappeared rapidly after contact with the Spanish because of overwork and the first <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> of European diseases, which struck Hispaniola after 1519.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-pest-118">[115]</a></sup> Las Casas records that when he first came to Hispaniola in 1508, "there were 60,000 people living on this island, including the Indians; so that from 1494 to 1508, over three million people had perished from war, slavery, and the mines. Who in future generations will believe this? I myself writing it as a knowledgeable eyewitness can hardly believe it...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-delaCasas_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-delaCasas-119">[116]</a></sup> Modern estimates for the pre-Columbian population of Hispaniola are around 250,000–300,000. According to the historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Fernandez_de_Oviedo_y_Valdes" title="Gonzalo Fernandez de Oviedo y Valdes">Gonzalo Fernandez de Oviedo y Valdes</a> by 1548, 56 years after Columbus landed, fewer than five hundred Taínos were left on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>The native Taíno people of the island were systematically enslaved via the <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a> system implemented by Columbus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> which resembled a feudal system in Medieval Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> Disease played a significant role in the destruction of the natives. Indirect evidence suggests that some serious illness may have arrived with the 1500 colonists who accompanied Columbus's second expedition in 1493. By the end of 1494, disease and famine had claimed two-thirds of the Spanish settlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pest_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-pest-118">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> When the first <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> finally struck in 1519 it wiped out much of the remaining native population.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Columbus's soldiers killed and enslaved with impunity at every landing. When Columbus fell ill in 1495, "what little restraint he had maintained over his men disappeared as he went through a lengthy period of recuperation. The troops went wild, stealing, killing, raping, and torturing natives, trying to force them to divulge the whereabouts of the imagined treasure-houses of gold." According to Las Casas, 50,000 natives perished during this period. Upon his recovery, Columbus organized his troops' efforts, forming a squadron of several hundred heavily armed men and more than twenty attack dogs. The men tore across the land, killing thousands of sick and unarmed natives. Soldiers would use their captives for sword practice, attempting to decapitate them or cut them in half with a single blow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Howard_Zinn" title="Howard Zinn">Howard Zinn</a> writes that Columbus spearheaded a massive slave trade; in 1495 his men captured in a single raid 1500 Arawak men, women, and children. When he shipped five hundred of the slaves to Spain, 40 percent died <i>en route</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-52">[49]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/James_W._Loewen" title="James W. Loewen">James W. Loewen</a> asserts that "Columbus not only sent the first slaves across the Atlantic, he probably sent more slaves – about five thousand – than any other individual... other nations rushed to emulate Columbus."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewen1995_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewen1995-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Las Casas writes that when slaves held in captivity began to die at high rates, Columbus switched to a different system of forced labor: he ordered all natives over the age of thirteen to collect a specified amount (one hawk's bell full) of gold powder every three months. Natives who brought the amount were given a copper token to hang around their necks, and those found without tokens had their hands amputated and were left to bleed to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koning_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koning-125">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Arawaks attempted to fight back against Columbus's men but lacked their armor, guns, swords, and horses. When taken prisoner, they were hanged or burned to death. Desperation led to mass suicides and infanticide among the natives. In just two years under Columbus's governorship more than half of the 250,000 Arawaks in Haiti were dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinn_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-Zinn-52">[49]</a></sup> The main cause for the depopulation was disease followed by other causes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> and harsh enslavement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbbot2010_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbbot2010-127">[124]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChrisp200634_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChrisp200634-128">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Eliot_Morison" title="Samuel Eliot Morison">Samuel Eliot Morison</a>, a Harvard historian and author of a multivolume biography on Columbus writes, "The cruel policy initiated by Columbus and pursued by his successors resulted in complete genocide."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morison_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morison-129">[126]</a></sup> Loewen laments that while "Haiti under the Spanish is one of the primary instances of genocide in all human history", only one major history text he reviewed mentions Columbus's role in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewen199563.E2.80.9364_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewen199563.E2.80.9364-130">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>However some of these accounts may be part of <a href="/wiki/Black_Legend" title="Black Legend">Black Legend</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup> Noble David Cook, writing about the <a href="/wiki/Black_Legend" title="Black Legend">Black Legend</a> and the conquest of the Americas wrote, "There were too few Spaniards to have killed the millions who were reported to have died in the first century after Old and New World contact". He instead estimates that the death toll was caused by diseases like smallpox,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook1998_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook1998-134">[131]</a></sup> which according to some estimates had an 80–90% fatality rate in Native American populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is evidence that the men of the first voyage also brought <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a> from the New World to Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup> Many of the crew members who served on this voyage later joined the army of <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">King Charles VIII</a> in his invasion of Italy in 1495. After the victory, Charles's largely mercenary army returned to their respective homes, thereby spreading "the Great Pox" across Europe and triggering the deaths of more than five million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="500" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg/200px-Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg/300px-Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg/400px-Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christopher_Columbus_Face.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Virgin_of_the_Navigators" title="The Virgin of the Navigators">The Virgin of the Navigators</a></i>, 1531–36</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although an abundance of artwork involving Christopher Columbus exists, no <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authentic</a> contemporary <a href="/wiki/Portrait" title="Portrait">portrait</a> has been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_W._Loewen" title="James W. Loewen">James W. Loewen</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/Lies_My_Teacher_Told_Me" title="Lies My Teacher Told Me">Lies My Teacher Told Me</a></i>, believes the various posthumous portraits have no historical value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewen199555_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewen199555-139">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sometime between 1531 and 1536, <a href="/wiki/Alejo_Fern%C3%A1ndez" title="Alejo Fernández">Alejo Fernández</a> painted an altarpiece, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Virgin_of_the_Navigators" title="The Virgin of the Navigators">The Virgin of the Navigators</a></i>, that includes a depiction of Columbus. The painting was commissioned for a chapel in Seville's <a href="/wiki/Casa_de_Contrataci%C3%B3n" title="Casa de Contratación">Casa de Contratación</a> (House of Trade) and remains there, as the earliest known painting about the discovery of the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the 1893 <a href="/wiki/World%27s_Columbian_Exposition" title="World's Columbian Exposition">World's Columbian Exposition</a>, 71 alleged portraits of Columbus were displayed; most did not match contemporary descriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup> These writings describe him as having reddish or blond hair, which turned to white early in his life, light colored eyes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[140]</a></sup> as well as being a lighter-skinned person with too much sun exposure turning his face red. Accounts consistently describe Columbus as a large and physically strong man of some six feet (1.83 metres) or more in height, easily taller than the average European of his day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most iconic image of Columbus is a portrait by <a href="/wiki/Sebastiano_del_Piombo" title="Sebastiano del Piombo">Sebastiano del Piombo</a>, which has been reproduced in many textbooks. It agrees with descriptions of Columbus in that it shows a large man with auburn hair, but the painting dates from 1519 and cannot, therefore, have been painted from life. Furthermore, the inscription identifying the subject as Columbus was probably added later, and the face shown differs from other images, including that of the "Virgin of the Navigators."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Met-Piombo_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Met-Piombo-145">[142]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Even with less than a complete record, however, scholars can state with assurance that Columbus was born in the republic of Genoa in northern Italy, although perhaps not in the city itself, and that his family made a living in the wool business as weavers and merchants. ... The two main early biographies of Columbus have been taken as literal truth by hundreds of writers, in large part because they were written by individuals closely connected to Columbus or his writings. ... Both biographies have serious shortcomings as evidence."</span></li>
